22:02:03","updatedTime":"2020-08-19 22:02:03","title":"How to Create a Custom WordPress Plugin From Scratch","link":"https://webdesign.tutsplus.com/tutorials/create-a-custom-wordpress-plugin-from-scratch--net-2668","description":"<p>Every WordPress site needs a theme to pull content from the database and display that in a design. And theoretically you could run a site with just a theme and nothing else. But that site would be very limited without the addition of plugins.<br></p><p>Plugins add extra functionality to your WordPress site over and above what comes with WordPress core. Everything from a booking calendar or animated slider, to a full-featured learning management system or online marketplace—you can add them all to your site with plugins. </p><p>In this guide, I’ll show you how to create your own WordPress plugin. I’ll show you how to use best practice in plugin development, how to get the code in your plugin to run, and how to structure your plugin’s code and files. I’ll also walk you through the process of creating your first plugin and answer some FAQs.</p><h4>Free and Paid WordPress Plugins</h4><p>If you want to add some specific features to your website, there are plenty of places you can buy or download plugins. The WordPress plugin directory includes thousands of free plugins that’ll help you create the site you need. But if you’re looking for more advanced features, a better user interface, or improved support, it’s worth buying premium plugins from authors on <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress\">CodeCanyon</a>.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"CodeCanyon\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/Code%20Canyon.jpg\"></a></figure><p>But sometimes you might need to code your own plugin! This can be more efficient than using a third-party plugin, as you might only need a part of the code provided by those. It also means you can develop a plugin that meets your needs more precisely, or you can customize an existing plugin to adapt it for your site.</p><h2><b>What Do You Need to Make a Plugin?</b></h2><p>To build your own plugin and run it on your WordPress site, you’ll need:<br></p><ul>\n<li>a code editor</li>\n<li>a development WordPress installation with a copy of your live site for testing</li>\n</ul><p>Don't test your plugin on your live site until you know it works!</p><p>If you don’t already have a local WordPress installation, follow our guide to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/articles/how-to-set-up-a-killer-wordpress-testing-environment-locally--net-5065\">copying your site to a local install</a>. Or if you can’t install WordPress locally, use a duplicate of your site on a testing installation on your server. Find out <a href=\"https://webdesign.tutsplus.com/tutorials/how-to-migrate-a-wordpress-site--cms-34827\">how to copy your site</a>.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\">\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/articles/how-to-set-up-a-killer-wordpress-testing-environment-locally--net-5065\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cdn.tutsplus.com/net/uploads/legacy/331_ultimateWP/200x200.png\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">How to Set Up a Killer WordPress Testing Environment Locally</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Harley Alexander</div></a></li>\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://webdesign.tutsplus.com/tutorials/how-to-migrate-a-wordpress-site--cms-34827\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/34827/preview_image/migrating%20wordpress.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-webdesign\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">How to Migrate a WordPress Site</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Rachel McCollin</div></a></li>\n</ul><h2><b>Types of WordPress Plugin</b></h2><p>Plugins can carry out lots of tasks. What they all have in common is that they add extra functionality to your site. Types of WordPress plugin include:</p><ul>\n<li>\n<strong>site maintenance plugins</strong> for things like security, performance, or backups</li>\n<li>\n<strong>marketing and sales plugins</strong> for things like SEO, social media, or eCommerce</li>\n<li>\n<strong>content plugins</strong> such as custom post types, widgets, shortcodes, forms, galleries, and video feeds</li>\n<li>\n<strong>API plugins</strong> that work with the WordPress REST API or pull in external content from services like Google Maps</li>\n<li>\n<strong>community plugins</strong> that add social networking features</li>\n</ul><p>…and lots more! For an idea of what plugins can do, check out the <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/\" target=\"_self\">WordPress plugin directory</a> and the <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress\">CodeCanyon</a> marketplace.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"WordPress Plugin Directory\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/plugin%20directory.jpg\"></a></figure><h2><b>What Goes Into a Plugin? </b></h2><p>Before you get started building your plugin, it’s worth knowing what goes into a plugin. Exactly what the plugin code will look like will depend on your plugin: some are small, with just one plugin file, while others are massive, with multiple include files, scripts, stylesheets and template files. And there are plenty that fall somewhere in the middle.</p><p>The elements you’ll probably have in your plugin are:</p><ul>\n<li>the main plugin file (this is essential)</li>\n<li>folders for different file types</li>\n<li>scripts</li>\n<li>stylesheets</li>\n<li>include files to organize the code</li>\n</ul><p>Let’s have a look at each of these.</p><h3><b>The Main Plugin File</b></h3><p>The main plugin file is essential. It will always be a PHP file, and it will always contain commented-out text that tells WordPress about your plugin.</p><p>Here’s an example, from the Akismet plugin:</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">&lt;?php\n/**\n * @package Akismet\n */\n/*\nPlugin Name: Akismet Anti-Spam\nPlugin URI: https://akismet.com/\nDescription: Used by millions, Akismet is quite possibly the best way in the world to &lt;strong&gt;protect your blog from spam&lt;/strong&gt;. It keeps your site protected even while you sleep. To get started: activate the Akismet plugin and then go to your Akismet Settings page to set up your API key.\nVersion: 4.1.6\nAuthor: Automattic\nAuthor URI: https://automattic.com/wordpress-plugins/\nLicense: GPLv2 or later\nText Domain: akismet\n*/</pre><p>This tells WordPress what your plugin does, where to find out more about it, and who developed it. It also gives information about the version number and the text domain and path for internationalisation, as well as the license.</p><p>WordPress takes this information and uses it to populate the plugins screen in your site. Here’s how Akismet looks on that screen:</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"Akismet in plugins screen\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/Akismet%20in%20plugins%20screen.jpg\"></figure><p>You can see that the information provided in the plugin file is used to populate this entry and provide links.</p><p>Other information about the plugin is contained in the <strong>README.txt</strong> file, which is used to populate the plugin's page in the plugin directory:</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"Akismet in plugin directory\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/Akismet%20in%20plugin%20directory.jpg\"></figure><p>The main plugin file will also contain the code that makes the plugin run. Sometimes that will be all the PHP for the plugin, but for larger plugins, there’ll be calls to include files containing extra code. This helps you organize your code and means you don’t have one long disorganized file that’s difficult to work with. I’ll show you how to use include files later in this guide.</p><h3><b>Folder Structure</b></h3><p>While there are no hard and fast rules on how you organize the folders in your plugin, it makes sense to adopt the same structure that other plugin developers use. This will familiarize you with the way other plugins are built and mean that if you share your code in future, it will make sense to other people.</p><p>Folders in your plugin might include:</p><ul>\n<li>\n<strong>css</strong> or <strong>styles</strong> for stylesheets</li>\n<li>\n<strong>scripts</strong> for JavaScript</li>\n<li>\n<strong>includes</strong> for include files</li>\n<li>\n<strong>templates</strong> for template files that your plugin outputs</li>\n<li>\n<strong>assets</strong> for media and other asset files</li>\n<li>\n<strong>i18n</strong> for internationalisation files</li>\n</ul><p>You might find you need to use more folders if your plugin is large or complex. For example, WooCommerce has folders for packages, sample data, and more. These in turn include subfolders for things like blocks and admin files.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"WooCommerce file structure\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/WooCommerce%20file%20structure.jpg\"></figure><h3><b>Scripts and Stylesheets</b></h3><p>If your plugin outputs content that needs styling, either in the front-end or in the admin screens, you may need stylesheets. And if your plugin will use scripts, you’ll need files for these.</p><p>It makes sense to keep these in their own folder, even if you only have one of each. You’ll need to enqueue these scripts and stylesheets using a dedicated function in your main plugin file. I’ll show you how to do this when we’re building the plugin.</p><h3><b>Include Files</b></h3><p>If your plugin needs organisation, you can do this by splitting your code into multiple files, called include files. You then put these files into their own folder and call them in your main plugin file using an <code class=\"inline\">include</code> or <code class=\"inline\">require</code> function.</p><p>This way, you can keep the bulk of your code in a well-organized file structure while your main plugin file remains lean and minimal.</p><p>If your plugin isn’t all that big, you don’t need to use include files: just add your code to the main plugin file. But you might find you need to organize this file and reorder functions within it as you add them, to maintain a logical structure.</p><p>These are the most common elements of a plugin. We’ve seen in the WooCommerce example that there can be many more. Or in smaller plugins there can be many fewer. But as you develop more plugins, you’ll find yourself using these elements more and more.</p><h2><b>How to Run Your Plugin Code: Options</b></h2><p>When you add code to your plugin, it won’t do anything until you activate it in some way. There are a few methods you can use to activate your code or pull in code from WordPress:</p><ul>\n<li>functions</li>\n<li>action and filter hooks</li>\n<li>classes</li>\n</ul><p>Let’s take a look each of these.</p><h3><b>Functions</b></h3><p>Functions are the building blocks of WordPress code. They’re the easiest way to get started writing your own plugins and the quickest to code. You’ll find plenty of them in your themes' files too.</p><p>Each function will have its own name, followed by braces and the code inside those braces.</p><p>The code inside your plugin won’t run unless you call the function somehow. The simplest (but least flexible) way to do that is by directly calling the code in your theme or somewhere else in your plugin.</p><p>Here’s an example function:</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">tutsplus_myfunction {\n // code goes here\n}</pre><p>To directly call that function in your theme, you’d simply type <code class=\"inline\">tutsplus_myfunction()</code> in the place in your theme template files where you want it to run. Or you might add it somewhere in your plugin... but you’d also need to activate the code that calls it!</p><p>There are a few limitations of this:</p><ul>\n<li>If the function does something that isn’t just adding content somewhere in a theme template file, you can’t activate it this way.</li>\n<li>If you want to call the function in multiple places, you’ll have to call it again and again.</li>\n<li>It can be hard to keep track of all the places you’ve manually called a function.</li>\n</ul><p>It’s much better practice to call functions by attaching them to a hook.</p><h3><b>Action and Filter Hooks</b></h3><p>By attaching your function to a hook, you run its code whenever that hook is fired. There are two types of hook: action hooks and filter hooks.</p><p>Action hooks are empty. When WordPress comes to them, it does nothing unless a function has been hooked to that hook.</p><p>Filter hooks contain code that will run <em>unless</em> there is a function hooked to that hook. If there is a function, it’ll run the code in that function instead. This means you can add default code to your plugin but override it in another plugin, or you can write a function that overrides default code that’s attached to a filter hook in WordPress itself.</p><p>Hooks are fired in three ways:</p><ul>\n<li>By WordPress itself. The WordPress core code includes hundreds of hooks that fire at different times. Which one you hook your function to will depend on what your function does and when you want its code to run. You can find a list of <a href=\"https://developer.wordpress.org/plugins/hooks/\">WordPress hooks</a> in the developer handbook.</li>\n<li>By your theme. Many themes include action and filter hooks that you can use to add extra content in key places in your website’s design. And all themes will include a <code class=\"inline\">wp_head</code> and <code class=\"inline\">wp_footer</code> hook. Combine these with conditional tags and you can run specific code on certain types of pages in your site.</li>\n<li>By your plugin or other plugins. You might add an action hook to your plugin and then add functions in your include files that attach code to that hook. Or you might write a filter hook and then have a function that overrides its contents under certain circumstances. Alternatively, if you’re creating a plugin to complement another plugin, you can hook your functions to the existing hook in the third-party plugin. I’ve done this with WooCommerce, for example, to customize what’s output on product pages.</li>\n</ul><p>Some of this is more advanced, but with your first plugin, you’ll probably be hooking your functions to an action or filter hook output by WordPress itself, most likely an action hook.</p><h3><b>Classes</b></h3><p>Classes are a way of coding more complex features such as widgets and customizer elements, that make use of the existing WordPress APIs. </p><p>When you write a class in your plugin, you’ll probably be extending an existing class that’s coded into WordPress. This way, you can make use of the code provided by the class and tweak it to make it your own. An example would be the customizer, where you might write a class including a color picker, making use of the color picker UI that’s provided in the existing class for the customizer.</p><p>Using classes is more advanced than functions, and it’s unlikely you’ll do it in your first plugin. To find out more, see our guide to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/object-oriented-programming-in-wordpress-classes--cms-20021\">classes in WordPress</a>.</p><p>If you do write classes, you’ll still have to use actions or filters to get them to run.</p><h2><b>Plugin Best Practices</b></h2><p>Before you start coding your plugin, it helps to understand <a href=\"https://developer.wordpress.org/plugins/plugin-basics/best-practices/\">best practices</a> for plugins so your code can be high quality right from the start. </p><p>These include:</p><ul>\n<li>Write your code according to <a href=\"https://make.wordpress.org/core/handbook/best-practices/coding-standards/\">WordPress coding standards</a>. If you want to submit your plugin to the plugin directory, you’ll have to do this.</li>\n<li>Use comments throughout your code so other people can work with it—and so you remember how your code works when you come back to it in the future.</li>\n<li>Name your functions, hooks, and classes using prefixes so they are unique to your plugin. You don’t want to give a function the same name as another function in a different plugin or in WordPress core.</li>\n<li>Organize your folders logically, and keep your code separated so other people can understand it and so you can add to it over time without it becoming a mess.</li>\n</ul><p>You might think that using best practice isn’t necessary because it’s just you working with the plugin. But your plugin might grow over time, you might let other people use it, or you might sell it. Or you might come back to it in two years and not be able to remember how the code is organized!</p><h2><b>Creating Your First Plugin in 4 Steps</b></h2><p>At last! You have a grounding in how plugins work, and it’s time to roll your sleeves up and create your first plugin. I’m going to take you through the process of creating a simple plugin that registers a custom post type.</p><p>This is a very common use of a plugin, and something you might then build on over time to add custom template files for your custom post type or other functionality.</p><p>I’ll show you the basic code for the plugin and give you an introduction to how you might add to it over time.</p><h3><b>1. Create the Plugin Folder and File</b></h3><p>Even if your plugin is starting out small with just one file, it’s good practice to give it its own folder. Start by creating a folder in your <strong>wp-content/plugins</strong> directory. Inside that, create a PHP file for your plugin.</p><p>Give them both a name that makes sense and includes a prefix. I’m calling my folder <strong>tutsplus-register-post-types</strong> and my file <strong>tutsplus-register-post-types.php</strong>.</p><p>Now open your plugin file and add the commented-out information at the top. You can take mine below and edit it to reflect the fact that this is your plugin, not mine.</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">&lt;?php\n/*\nPlugin Name: Tuts+ Register Post Types\nPlugin URI: https://tutsplus.com/\nDescription: Plugin to accompany tutsplus guide to creating plugins, registers a post type.\nVersion: 1.0\nAuthor: Rachel McCollin\nAuthor URI: https://rachelmccollin.com/\nLicense: GPLv2 or later\nText Domain: tutsplus\n*/</pre><p>Now, if you save your file and go to the <strong>Plugins</strong> screen in your development site, you’ll see the plugin on the screen:</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"Our plugin on the Plugins screen\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/Our%20plugin%20on%20the%20Plugins%20screen.jpg\"></figure><p>You can activate it if you want, but it won’t do anything yet because you haven’t added any code to it. Let’s do that.</p><h3><b>2. Add Functions</b></h3><p>Now it’s time to write the first function in our plugin. Start by making your plugin and adding the braces which will contain the code. Here’s mine:</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">function tutsplus_register_post_type() {\n    \n\t// movies\n\t$labels = array( \n\t\t'name' =&gt; __( 'Movies' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'singular_name' =&gt; __( 'Movie' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'add_new' =&gt; __( 'New Movie' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'add_new_item' =&gt; __( 'Add New Movie' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'edit_item' =&gt; __( 'Edit Movie' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'new_item' =&gt; __( 'New Movie' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'view_item' =&gt; __( 'View Movie' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'search_items' =&gt; __( 'Search Movies' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'not_found' =&gt;  __( 'No Movies Found' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'not_found_in_trash' =&gt; __( 'No Movies found in Trash' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t);\n\t$args = array(\n\t\t'labels' =&gt; $labels,\n\t\t'has_archive' =&gt; true,\n\t\t'public' =&gt; true,\n\t\t'hierarchical' =&gt; false,\n\t\t'supports' =&gt; array(\n\t\t\t'title', \n\t\t\t'editor', \n\t\t\t'excerpt', \n\t\t\t'custom-fields', \n\t\t\t'thumbnail',\n\t\t\t'page-attributes'\n\t\t),\n\t\t'rewrite'   =&gt; array( 'slug' =&gt; 'movies' ),\n\t\t'show_in_rest' =&gt; true\n\n\t);\n\tregister_post_type( 'tutsplus_movie', $args );\n\t\t\n}</pre><p>This includes all the labels and arguments for your post type and (crucially) the <code class=\"inline\">register_post_type()</code> function which is provided by WordPress.</p><p>I’ve used <code class=\"inline\">movies</code> as my post type here as I’m creating an imaginary movie review site. You might want to use something different.</p><p>Now, if you save your file and go back to your site, you’ll see that nothing has changed. That’s because you haven’t activated your code. The method we use to activate the function here is by hooking it to an action hook provided by WordPress, the <code class=\"inline\">init</code> hook. When you use a function provided by WordPress (such as <code class=\"inline\">register_post_type</code>), you’ll find that there’s a hook that you should use. You can find details in the WordPress handbook entry for <a href=\"https://developer.wordpress.org/reference/functions/register_post_type/\">registering custom post types</a>.</p><p>So let’s add the hook. Under your code, and outside the braces, add this line:</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">add_action( 'init', 'tutsplus_register_post_type' );</pre><p>We use the <code class=\"inline\">add_action()</code> function to hook our code to an action hook, with two parameters: the name of the action hook and the name of our function.</p><p>Now try saving your files and going back to your site. You’ll see that the custom post type has been added to your admin menu.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"custom post type in menu\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/custom%20post%20type%20in%20menu.jpg\"></figure><p>Nice!</p><p>Now let’s add an extra function, to register a custom taxonomy. Below the code you’ve written so far, add this:</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">function tutsplus_register_taxonomy() {    \n\t\n\t// books\n\t$labels = array(\n\t\t'name' =&gt; __( 'Genres' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'singular_name' =&gt; __( 'Genre', 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'search_items' =&gt; __( 'Search Genres' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'all_items' =&gt; __( 'All Genres' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'edit_item' =&gt; __( 'Edit Genre' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'update_item' =&gt; __( 'Update Genres' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'add_new_item' =&gt; __( 'Add New Genre' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'new_item_name' =&gt; __( 'New Genre Name' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t\t'menu_name' =&gt; __( 'Genres' , 'tutsplus' ),\n\t);\n\t\n\t$args = array(\n\t\t'labels' =&gt; $labels,\n\t\t'hierarchical' =&gt; true,\n\t\t'sort' =&gt; true,\n\t\t'args' =&gt; array( 'orderby' =&gt; 'term_order' ),\n\t\t'rewrite' =&gt; array( 'slug' =&gt; 'genres' ),\n\t\t'show_admin_column' =&gt; true,\n\t\t'show_in_rest' =&gt; true\n\n\t);\n\t\n\tregister_taxonomy( 'tutsplus_genre', array( 'tutsplus_movie' ), $args);\n\t\n}\nadd_action( 'init', 'tutsplus_register_taxonomy' );\n</pre><p>Again, you might want to change the name of your custom taxonomy. Here, I’ve made the taxonomy apply to the post type I’ve just registered (the third parameter of the <code class=\"inline\">register_taxonomy</code> function). If you gave your post type a different name, make sure to edit that bit.</p><p>Now save your file and take a look at your admin screens. When you hover over your post type in the admin menu, you’ll see the new taxonomy.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"custom taxonomy in admin menu\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/custom%20taxonomy%20in%20admin%20menu.jpg\"></figure><p>You now have a working plugin. Well done!</p><p>Let’s take a look at how you might add to it.</p><h3><b>3. Enqueue Stylesheets and Scripts</b></h3><p>If you need to use custom styling or scripts in your plugin, you could add them right into your plugin file—but that’s not best practice. Instead, you should create stylesheets and scripts as separate files in your plugin folder and enqueue those, using a function provided by WordPress.</p><p>Let’s imagine you want to add styling for your custom post type. You could add this to your theme, but you might want to add some specific styling to the plugin to make the post type stand out from other post types in any theme.</p><p>To do this, you create a new folder inside your plugin folder called <strong>css</strong> (or <strong>styles</strong>, it’s up to you). Inside that folder, create a stylesheet called <strong>style.css</strong>, or you can give it a more specific name for clarity. I’m going to call mine <strong>movies.css</strong>.</p><p>You then need to enqueue that file in your plugin so that it can be used by WordPress. Add this to your main plugin file, above the functions you already have. I like to add enqueuing and includes first in my plugin file so I can see what other files are being activated.</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">function tutsplus_movie_styles() {\n    wp_enqueue_style( 'movies',  plugin_dir_url( __FILE__ ) . ‘/css/movies.css’ );                      \n}\nadd_action( 'wp_enqueue_scripts', ‘tutsplus_movie_styles' );</pre><p>If you save your file, you won’t see any difference in your admin screens—but if you’ve added posts of the custom post type and your stylesheet includes styling for them, you’ll now see that in the front-end of your site.</p><p>Note that the hook used for enqueuing both stylesheets and scripts is the same: they both use <code class=\"inline\">wp_enqueue_scripts</code>. There isn’t a separate hook for styles.</p><p>Enqueuing scripts works in a very similar way. Follow these steps:</p><ul>\n<li>Add a <strong>scripts</strong> or <strong>js</strong> folder to your plugin folder.</li>\n<li>Save your script files in that folder.</li>\n<li>Enqueue the script in the same way as the stylesheet above, replacing the <code class=\"inline\">wp_enqueue_style()</code> function with <code class=\"inline\">wp_enqueue_script()</code>.</li>\n</ul><h3><b>4. Using Include Files</b></h3><p>Another option as you develop your plugin is to create extra PHP files, known as include files. If you have a lot of these, you might create multiple folders for different types of include file, or you might just create one folder called <strong>includes</strong>.</p><p>There are a few functions you can use to include files, which you’ll find in our comprehensive guide to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/articles/how-to-include-and-require-files-and-templates-in-wordpress--wp-26419\">including and requiring files</a>.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\"><li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/articles/how-to-include-and-require-files-and-templates-in-wordpress--wp-26419\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cdn.tutsplus.com/wp/authors/legacy/Japh/2012/08/27/preview.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">Creative Coding</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">How to Include and Require Files and Templates in WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Tom McFarlin</div></a></li></ul><p>For example, in our custom post type plugin, we might create some code to vary the way the content of the page is output, using the <code class=\"inline\">the_content</code> filter hook to amend the code being run each time the content is output on a product page.</p><p>Instead of adding this code to the main plugin file, you could add it to a separate file called <strong>movie-content.php</strong> and then write the code in that file for the way the content is output for movies.</p><p>To include this file in your plugin, you add a folder called <strong>includes</strong> to your plugin, and then inside that folder you add the <strong>content-movie.php</strong> file.</p><p>To include that file in your plugin, you add this code at the beginning for the main plugin file:</p><pre class=\"brush: php noskimlinks noskimwords\">include( plugin_dir_path( __FILE__ ) . ‘includes/movie-content.php' );</pre><p>You don’t need to hook this to an action or filter hook—just use the <code class=\"inline\">include_once()</code> function in your plugin file. That will then call the code from the include file as if it was in your main plugin file at that point.</p><h2><b>Taking It Further: Plugin Features</b></h2><p>In this guide, I’ve shown you how plugins are coded and how to get started building a simple plugin.</p><p>Once you’ve got the hang of plugin development, you can build more complex and full-featured plugins to carry out more complex tasks on your site.</p><p>Let’s take a look at some examples.</p><h3><b>Widget Plugins</b></h3><p>Widget plugins involve working with classes but are a good introduction to the topic. Our guide to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/creating-your-own-widgets-using-various-wordpress-apis-introduction--cms-22460\">creating a widget plugin</a> will help you do it.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\"><li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/creating-your-own-widgets-using-various-wordpress-apis-introduction--cms-22460\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/411/posts/22460/preview_image/Creating_Your_Own_Widgets_400px.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress Widgets</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Creating Your Own Widgets Using Various WordPress APIs: Introduction</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Bonang Salemane</div></a></li></ul><p>You can also find plenty of <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress/widgets\">widget plugins</a> on CodeCanyon, which will save you coding them yourself. In our blog, we’ve identified the <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/wordpress-widgets-to-watch--cms-27752\">best widget plugins for 2020</a>, the <a href=\"https://webdesign.tutsplus.com/tutorials/10-best-wordpress-facebook-widgets--cms-27289\">best Facebook widgets</a>, and the <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/10-best-wordpress-twitter-widgets--cms-27127\">best Twitter widgets</a>.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\"><li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/wordpress-widgets-to-watch--cms-27752\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/1225/posts/27752/preview_image/wordpress%20ness.png\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Best WordPress Widgets for 2020</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Daniel Strongin</div></a></li></ul><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"Code Canyon widget plugins\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/Code%20Canyon%20widget%20plugins.jpg\"></figure><h3><b>Shortcode Plugins</b></h3><p>Creating a shortcode is a great place to start creating plugins as they’re relatively simple and very useful. Find out how to create them with our guide to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/articles/getting-started-with-wordpress-shortcodes--wp-21197\">coding shortcodes in WordPress</a>.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\"><li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/articles/getting-started-with-wordpress-shortcodes--wp-21197\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cdn.tutsplus.com/wp/uploads/legacy/049_GettingStartedWPShortcodes/preview.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">Plugins</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Getting Started With WordPress Shortcodes</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Rohan Mehta</div></a></li></ul><p>CodeCanyon also has a bunch of useful <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress?term=wordpress%20shortcodes&amp;view=grid#content\">shortcode plugins</a> you can use to add extra functionality to your site.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress?term=wordpress%20shortcodes&amp;view=grid#content\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"Code Canyon shortcode plugins\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/Code%20Canyon%20shortcode%20plugins.jpg\"></a></figure><h3><b>Social Media Plugins</b></h3><p>Social media plugins are incredibly popular as they let you display your Facebook, Twitter, or Instagram feed on your site and let your visitors share your content via their own social media accounts.</p><p>CodeCanyon has dozens of <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress?term=wordpress%20social%20media&amp;view=grid\">social media plugins</a>. Learn about the <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/best-wordpress-social-media-plugins--cms-32781\">best social media plugins</a> and how to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/add-an-online-community-to-your-wordpress-site-with-userpro--cms-33606\">create an online community for your WordPress site</a>.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\">\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/best-wordpress-social-media-plugins--cms-32781\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/1809/posts/32781/preview_image/Untitled-1.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress Plugins</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Best WordPress Social Media Plugins of 2019</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Daniel Strongin</div></a></li>\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/add-an-online-community-to-your-wordpress-site-with-userpro--cms-33606\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/33606/preview_image/create-your-own-online-community-with-userpro.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Create an Online Community for Your WordPress Site With UserPro</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Jessica Thornsby</div></a></li>\n</ul><h3><b>Gallery and Media Plugins</b></h3><p>If you want to optimize the media on your site and display galleries or video feeds, a plugin will make everything look more professional. Find out how to find the <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/how-to-find-the-best-wordpress-gallery-plugin-for-images-or-video--cms-33638\">best gallery plugins for images or video</a> and how to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/create-a-wordpress-image-gallery-code-the-plugin--cms-32093\">code your own gallery plugin</a>. Or browse the professional <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress?term=wordpress%20image%20video&amp;view=grid\">gallery and video plugins</a> on CodeCanyon.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\">\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/how-to-find-the-best-wordpress-gallery-plugin-for-images-or-video--cms-33638\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/769/posts/33638/preview_image/galleries.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">How to Find the Best WordPress Gallery Plugins for Images or Video</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Franc Lucas</div></a></li>\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/create-a-wordpress-image-gallery-code-the-plugin--cms-32093\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/32093/preview_image/child%20pages.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Create a WordPress Image Gallery: Code the Plugin</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Rachel McCollin</div></a></li>\n</ul><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress?term=wordpress%20image%20video&amp;view=grid\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"media plugins on Code Canyon\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/media%20plugins%20on%20Code%20Canyon.jpg\"></a></figure><h3><b>Form Plugins</b></h3><p>Adding forms to your site lets your visitors get in touch and helps build a relationship. CodeCanyon has plenty of <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress?term=wordpress%20form&amp;view=grid\">premium form plugins</a> that will make it easy for your visitors to contact you. Learn <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/how-to-create-a-form-with-the-quform-wordpress-plugin--cms-33602\">how to create a form with the bestselling QuForm plugin</a>.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\"><li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/how-to-create-a-form-with-the-quform-wordpress-plugin--cms-33602\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/769/posts/33602/preview_image/forms.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress Plugins</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">How to Create a Form With the Quform WordPress Plugin </div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Daniel Strongin</div></a></li></ul><h2><b>FAQs</b></h2><p>Here are the answers to some of the most frequently asked questions about WordPress plugins.</p><p class=\"question\"><b>Why can’t I just add the code I need to my theme functions file?</b></p><p>It’s tempting to simply keep on adding code to the <strong>functions.php</strong> file, and there is some code that should be there.</p><p>But if your code is related to functionality in your site, rather than the design or the output of content, then you should code it into a plugin. This means that if you switch themes in the future, you still have that functionality. And you can use the plugin on another site running a different theme.</p><p class=\"question\"><b>I’ve added code to my plugin. Why is nothing happening?</b></p><p>This is probably because you haven’t hooked your code to an action or filter hook. Until you do that, nothing will happen.</p><p class=\"question\"><b>When I edit my plugin and check my site, I get a white screen. Help!</b></p><p>You’ve probably added some code that’s got an error in it somewhere. PHP is an unforgiving language, and this might be as minor as a semicolon in the wrong place.</p><p>Try <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/fixing-the-wordpress-white-screen-of-death--cms-21008\">turning on <code class=\"inline\">WP_DEBUG</code></a> in your <strong>wp-config.php</strong> file, and you’ll see a message telling you where the error is. Then you can fix it.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--list\"><li class=\"roundup-block__content\"><a class=\"roundup-block__content-link\" href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/fixing-the-wordpress-white-screen-of-death--cms-21008\"><img class=\"roundup-block__preview-image\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/34/posts/21008/preview_image/wp-error.jpg\"><div class=\"roundup-block__primary-category topic-code\">WordPress</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__content-title\">Fixing the WordPress White Screen of Death</div>\n<div class=\"roundup-block__author\">Sam B.</div></a></li></ul><p class=\"question\"><b>When I activate my plugin, I get an error message telling me too many headers have been output. What does this mean?</b></p><p>All this normally means is that there are too many empty lines in your plugin file. Go back and check there are no empty lines at the beginning of the file.</p><p>If that doesn’t fix it, try <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/fixing-the-wordpress-white-screen-of-death--cms-21008\" target=\"_self\">turning on </a><code class=\"inline\"><a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/fixing-the-wordpress-white-screen-of-death--cms-21008\" target=\"_self\">WP_DEBUG</a></code>.</p><p class=\"question\"><b>Where can I find out more about developing plugins?</b></p><p>We have lots of tutorials and courses helping you to <a href=\"https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/search/wordpress+plugin\">build all kinds of plugins</a> here on Envato Tuts+. See if you can find anything that inspires you.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"tutsplus plugins courses\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/227/posts/35454/image/tutsplus%20plugins%20courses.jpg\"></figure><p class=\"question\"><b>Where can I download plugins for my site?</b></p><p>You can choose from thousands of free plugins in the WordPress plugin directory. You can also buy third-party plugins from <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress\">CodeCanyon</a>. Always buy plugins from a reputable supplier so you can be sure they follow WordPress coding standards and don’t contain malicious code.</p><h2><b>Summary</b></h2><p>Plugins will turn your site from a simple blog into a powerful website that includes advanced features and is secure and robust. Try adding plugins to your site today and coding your own to see how you can use plugins to improve your WordPress site.</p><h2>The Best WordPress Themes and Plugins on Envato Market</h2><p>Explore thousands of the <a href=\"https://themeforest.net/popular_item/by_category?category=wordpress\" target=\"_self\">best WordPress themes ever created on ThemeForest</a> and <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/popular_item/by_category?category=wordpress\" target=\"_self\">leading WordPress plugins on CodeCanyon</a>. Purchase these high-quality WordPress themes and plugins and improve your website experience for you and your visitors. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a You could restrict access to selected pages and then sell subscriptions to this premium content or you could turn your entire website into an exclusive, members-only portal. Or perhaps you already have an offline community and want a way to manage it online.</p><p>Whatever your motivation, the quickest and easiest way to build an online community is to use a professionally developed WordPress membership plugin.</p><p>To help you get your membership site up and running, here are ten of the best WordPress membership plugins that are currently trending on CodeCanyon.</p><h3>1. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/final-user-wp-frontend-user-profiles/22231050\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Final User - WP Front-end User Profiles</a> </h3><p>The Final User WordPress membership plugin is very user friendly and a great place to kick off our list. It's a tool that you can use for WordPress membership management with its front-end dashboard and Mailchimp integration. </p><p>Final User comes with a high-quality payment gateway. It's compatible with Paypal, Stripe, and of course WooCommerce. Members can set up free accounts and trials, as well as one-time and recurring payments. If you're looking for the best membership plugin for WordPress, give Final User a try. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/final-user-wp-frontend-user-profiles/22231050\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"Final User WordPress Membership Plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/2660/posts/35597/image/final-user-wp-frontend-user-profiles-22231050.jpg\"></a></figure><h3>2. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/fitness-trainer-training-membership-plugin/19901278\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Fitness Trainer- Training Membership Plugin</a>\n</h3><p>As of January 2020, 93 percent of websites were using <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/search/woocommerce?utf8=%E2%9C%93\" target=\"_self\">WooCommerce</a> instead of an alternate WordPress plugin. If your website is one of these millions, then you can transform your existing WooCommerce installation into a WordPress membership website using this dedicated WordPress gym membership plugin. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/fitness-trainer-training-membership-plugin/19901278\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"Fitness Trainer WordPress Gym Membership Plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/2660/posts/35597/image/envato-market-fitness-trainer-training-membership-plugin-19901278.jpg\"></a></figure><p>Using this member list plugin for WordPress, you can:</p><ul>\n<li>create multiple fitness plans</li>\n<li>set up pricing tables</li>\n<li>accept PayPal and Stripe payments</li>\n<li>translate into 15 languages</li>\n</ul><p>If you already have WooCommerce set up or you plan to use it in the future, then you can save yourself a ton of time and effort by choosing <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/fitness-trainer-training-membership-plugin/19901278\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Fitness Trainer</a> as your WordPress gym membership plugin.</p><h3>3. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/user-profiles-made-easy-wordpress-plugin/4109874\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">User Profiles Made Easy - WordPress Plugin</a>\n</h3><p>The User Profiles Made Easy (UPME) plugin does exactly what it says on the tin: it makes user profiles easy! </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/user-profiles-made-easy-wordpress-plugin/4109874?_ga=2.238825798.71199192.1575379090-1280144257.1574810364\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"UPME Member List Plugin WordPress\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/User-Profiles-Made-Easy-upme%20.jpg\"></a><figcaption>UPME takes your profiles to the next level, by allowing you to create unlimited custom profile fields.</figcaption></figure><p>UPME has all the features you’d expect from a profile, login and restriction WordPress membership management plugin, plus a few interesting extras:</p><ul>\n<li>A seamless front-experience with profile, registration and login pages that can be customized to match the rest of your website. </li>\n<li>The ability to generate invitation codes and send them to specific users via email, allowing you to create an exclusive, invitation-only registration process.</li>\n<li>Advanced content restriction, including preventing non-registered visitors from accessing selected posts, pages, URLs, categories, content blocks, or even your entire website.</li>\n<li>One-click social login and registration thanks to seamless Facebook, Twitter and LinkedIn integration. You can also use UPME to sync content from these popular social networks to your website. </li>\n<li>Integration with WooCommerce and bbPress. </li>\n<li>7 free add-ons.</li>\n<li>All support queries are handled by <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/user-profiles-made-easy-wordpress-plugin/4109874?_ga=2.238825798.71199192.1575379090-1280144257.1574810364\">User Profiles Made Easy’s</a> lead developer. </li>\n</ul><h3>4. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-membership-pro-wordpress-plugin/12159253\" target=\"_self\">Ultimate Membership Pro WordPress Plugin</a>\n</h3><p>Ultimate Membership Pro (UMP) is a plugin that lets you sell membership subscriptions to your WordPress content. Once you’ve created one or more subscription levels, you can restrict access to your website’s content based on the visitor’s specific subscription level (or <em>lack</em> of subscription!).</p><p>This robust WordPress membership plugin integrates with all of the most popular payment and checkout systems, including PayPal, Stripe, and 2Checkout. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-membership-pro-wordpress-plugin/12159253\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"Ultimate Membership Pro Best Membership Plugin WordPress\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/ultimate-membership-pro-paypal-wordpress.png\"></a><figcaption>The UMP plugin supports all the major payment gateways.</figcaption></figure><p>If you’re a fan of the WooCommerce ecommerce platform, then you can also use UMP to restrict access to <em>any</em> WooCommerce product.</p><p><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-membership-pro-wordpress-plugin/12159253\">UMP</a> is a robust plugin for a WordPress membership site that doesn’t hold anything back—when you purchase this plugin, you’ll get 35 add-on packs included! </p><h3>5. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/armember-complete-wordpress-membership-system/17785056\" target=\"_self\">ARMember Premium Membership Plugin</a>\n</h3><p>ARMember is a one-stop solution that has everything you need to create a WordPress membership website. If you want the best membership plugin for WordPress, you'll like ARMember's impressive features.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/armember-complete-wordpress-membership-system/17785056\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"ARMember Premium WordPress Membership Plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/ARMember-membership-plugin.jpg\"></a><figcaption>ARMember has everything subscribers need to build a rich, multimedia profile. </figcaption></figure><p>Some of the ARMember WordPress membership plugin’s stand-out features include: </p><ul>\n<li>\n<strong>Automatically generated shortcode</strong>. Add plan selection, registration, payment and coupon creation functionality to your website with a <em>single</em> piece of shortcode. </li>\n<li>\n<strong>Multiple memberships</strong>. A single user can hold multiple membership plans and add-on options simultaneously.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Invite-only membership</strong>. Want to create an exclusive, invite-only website? Or perhaps you simply want more control over who uses your website? With ARMember, you can restrict sign-up so that users must receive an invite before they can register.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Grace period</strong>. If someone misses a payment or their membership expires, then you can allow them to continue accessing your content for a set period of time.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Flexible payment options</strong>. ARMember lets users choose between making a manual payment every time their membership runs out or setting up an auto-debit payment. </li>\n<li>\n<strong>Country-specific tax</strong>. You can define different tax percentages for different countries, and these settings will then be applied automatically based on the country the user selects when purchasing their membership. </li>\n<li>\n<strong>Built-in Security</strong>. ARMember helps protect your website with a range of security features, including brute force protection, IP and URL-based restriction, and the option to ban specific usernames and email addresses at sign-up.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Lots of integrations</strong>. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/armember-complete-wordpress-membership-system/17785056\">ARMember</a> supports eight different email tools, including Mailchimp, Mailster, and ActiveCampaign. It also integrates with MyCRED, BBpress, WooCommerce, Giphy, Mailchimp, and Mailster Newsletter. </p><h3>7. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/userpro-user-profiles-with-social-login/5958681?_ga=2.209361236.71199192.1575379090-1280144257.1574810364\" target=\"_self\">UserPro—Community Plugin With Social Login</a>\n</h3><p>UserPro provides everything you need to create a flourishing online community, including user profiles, login, registration, and content restriction.</p><p>User Imtovi says:</p><blockquote>“The most useful and powerful user profile Wordpress solution I have seen!” </blockquote><p>UserPro integrates with all the major social networks, including Facebook, Twitter, Instagram, and LinkedIn, so visitors can register with your website using their existing social media credentials. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/userpro-user-profiles-with-social-login/5958681?_ga=2.209361236.71199192.1575379090-1280144257.1574810364\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"UserPro Best Membership Plugin WordPress\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/userpro-social-login-facebook-twitter.png\"></a><figcaption>UserPro makes it easy to add front-end profiles, login and registration to your WordPress website.</figcaption></figure><p>When a new user logs in to their account, UserPro can sync their existing social media profiles with their new UserPro profile, helping them create an interesting and engaging profile with minimum effort.</p><blockquote>“I've been using this plugin for years. SUMO Memberships is a comprehensive WooCommerce membership system that lets you sell memberships through your existing WooCommerce store.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/sumo-memberships-woocommerce-membership-system/16642362\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"SUMO Membership Management\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/SUMO-Memberships-woocommerce.png\"></a><figcaption>SUMO Memberships is a complete WooCommerce Membership System.</figcaption></figure><p>Using the SUMO WordPress membership plugin, you can restrict access to pages, posts, content blocks, URLs, and custom post types, <em>and</em> grant access to specific WooCommerce products based on a user’s membership level. You can also provide alternate content for webpages that a user doesn’t have permission to view, such as an alternate page or post. </p><p>By providing alternate content for people who aren’t subscribed, you can provide a better user experience for <em>everyone</em> who visits your website. It's why many customers think that SUMO is the best membership plugin for WordPress for their WordPress membership sites.</p><blockquote>“Excellent support and willingness to adopt feature requests. Multiple membership plans can also be linked to the same account, so a single user can hold several membership plans simultaneously. </p><p>SUMO WordPress membership plugin user AssetDrop says:</p><blockquote>\"Changing systems over to SUMO completely was the best decision we could have made. They have more features, more flexibility and fantastic levels of support. I'm incredibly happy we found them and recommend them to anyone who wants to offer subscriptions, reward points, or discounts.” <br>\n</blockquote><h3>9. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/privatecontent-multilevel-content-plugin/1467885\" target=\"_self\">PrivateContent—Multilevel Content Plugin</a>\n</h3><p>You can use PrivateContent to quickly and easily transform your WordPress website into a multi-level membership platform by restricting access to every aspect of your site. </p><p>Some of this plugin’s standout features include:</p><ul><li>\n<strong>Advanced user tracking</strong>. Stop wondering what users are doing on your website! With PrivateContent’s native <a href=\"https://analytics.google.com/\">Google Analytics</a> integration, you can track every page view, login, logout, and registration in real time.</li></ul><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/privatecontent-multilevel-content-plugin/1467885\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"PrivateContent Member List Plugin WordPress\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/PrivateContent-wordpress-plugin-google-analytics%20.png\"></a><figcaption>PrivateContent records every pageview, login, logout, registration, and more, via its Google Analytics integration. </figcaption></figure><ul>\n<li>\n<strong>Restrict any element</strong>. PrivateContent can restrict menus, comments, widgets, posts, pages, categories, URLs, custom post types, custom taxonomies, and more. To make content restriction as easy as possible, restrictions are <em>inherited</em>—for example, if you restrict a parent category, then PrivateContent will automatically restrict all of its child categories. By taking advantage of inherited restrictions, you can define all of your website’s premium content in <em>seconds</em>. </li>\n<li>\n<strong>Forms blend seamlessly into your website</strong>. PrivateContent has three preset skins, but if none of these default skins quite meets your requirements, then PrivateContent also has a built-in skin builder.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Integration with Elementor, Visual Composer, and Cornerstone</strong>. Stop struggling to decipher complex shortcodes, and start using real-time previews.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Extreme mode</strong>. Lock down your entire website, including all menus, with a single click—even search engines won’t be able to find your site!</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Log in from any WP-based system</strong>. PrivateContent users are synced with WordPress users, so visitors can log in to your site using <em>any</em> WP-based system, including the popular WooCommerce platform.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Developer-friendly</strong>. Extend PrivateContent by taking advantage of the powerful PHP API, which features dozens of actions and filters. </li>\n<li>\n<strong>Completely translatable</strong>. PrivateContent has already been translated into 23 languages.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Anti-spam systems</strong>. This plugin supports honeypot and invisible reCAPTCHA.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Fast and reliable support</strong>. If you encounter any issues with PrivateContent, then you’ll have easy access to the help you need—the PrivateContent team typically answer support queries in less than 12 hours, 7 days a week. </li>\n</ul><p>The <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/privatecontent-multilevel-content-plugin/1467885\">PrivateContent: Multilevel Content Plugin</a> is a serious contender for anyone who wants to create a WordPress membership website. </p><h3>10. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-learning-pro-wordpress-plugin/21772657\" target=\"_self\">Ultimate Learning Pro—Learning Management System (LMS)</a> </h3><p>Do you have a portfolio of e-learning materials that you want to monetize? Or perhaps you have some specialist knowledge that you want to share with the world by creating your own online training course? </p><p>Ultimate Learning Pro is a membership plugin that’s targeted squarely at students and instructors. This plugin has everything you need to create, publish, distribute, and manage your own online courses. </p><p>Once students enroll with your website, they can use Ultimate Learning Pro’s extensive feature set to purchase, complete and review all of your e-learning materials.</p><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-learning-pro-wordpress-plugin/21772657\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"Ultimate Learning Pro Best Membership Plugin WordPress\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/Ultimate-Learning-Pro-WordPress-Plugin-LMS-elearning.png\"></a><figcaption>The Ultimate Learning Pro LMS helps you create a premium elearning platform for your students and instructors. </figcaption></figure><p>After completing each course, you can use this plugin to issue students with certificates, badges, and reward points, and gather feedback about their experiences with your e-learning materials.</p><p>For instructors, Ultimate Learning Pro provides all the tools required to manage a class of online students, including posting announcements and pushing notifications to students’ smartphones to ensure they never miss an important event or deadline. Ultimate Learning Pro also has a drip content feature that makes it easy to release lessons at predefined intervals. </p><p>Ultimate Learning Pro supports all the major payment services, including PayPal, Stripe, and 2CheckOut. When you purchase Ultimate Learning Pro, you’ll also get access to 20 add-ons, including WooCommerce, Ultimate Membership Pro, Easy Digital Downloads, and EDD payment integration.</p><p>Don't already have a portfolio of e-learning materials? <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-learning-pro-wordpress-plugin/21772657\">Ultimate Learning Pro</a> also features an intuitive course builder that makes it easy to start building your own online courses.</p><h3>11. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/-wpgym-wordpress-gym-management-system/13352964\" target=\"_self\">WPGYM<em></em>—WordPress Gym Management System</a> </h3><p>Unlike all the plugins we’ve covered so far, WordPress Gym Management System (WPGYM) is designed to help you manage an offline community, online. Specifically, it’s a complete WordPress gym membership plugin. </p><p>For gym owners and instructors, WPGYM provides all the tools you need to share nutrition plans, workout schedules, and class timetables, while gym members can use WPGYM to perform useful tasks such as tracking their physical stats over time or reserving their spot at an upcoming exercise class.</p><blockquote>“What a great team to work with, they always strive to make sure I'm happy and go the extra mile,\" says WPGYM user brokenbox. </blockquote><p>WPGYM includes the following modules: </p><ul><li>\n<strong>Gym member module</strong>. Here, you can track the progress of all your gym members, including their vital statistics. </li></ul><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/-wpgym-wordpress-gym-management-system/13352964\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"WPGYM WordPress Gym Membership Plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/WPGYM-gym-membership-plugin.jpg\"></a><figcaption>You can use WPGYM to create a complete, WordPress-powered gym management system.</figcaption></figure><ul><li>\n<strong>Workout module</strong>. Instructors can create a workout schedule for individual members, including unique sets, reps, and weight for each activity, and define a date range for each workout. The gym subscriber can then view their assigned workout and log their daily progress for their instructor to review.</li></ul><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/-wpgym-wordpress-gym-management-system/13352964\" target=\"_self\"><img alt=\"WPGYM WordPress Gym Membership Plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/WPGYM-membership-workout-module.jpg\"></a><figcaption>Instructors can create and distribute complete workout plans using the WPGYM plugin. </figcaption></figure><ul>\n<li>\n<strong>Nutrition module</strong>. Instructors can prepare and assign nutrition plans to individual gym members.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Attendance module</strong>. Here, you can manage the attendance of your staff and gym members and monitor the attendance for each class.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Facility management module</strong>. Record all the facilities available at your gym, reserve these facilities for various events and classes, and manage reservations for each facility.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Message module</strong>. Send messages to all staff and gym subscribers—handy if you ever need to notify subscribers about a cancelled or rescheduled class.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Newsletter module</strong>. WPGYM integrates with Mailchimp, so you can quickly and easily push a newsletter to all of your gym members using Mailchimp’s newsletter system.</li>\n<li>\n<strong>Accountant module</strong>. This is where you can manage your income, expenses, and membership fees. You can also use <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/ultimate-learning-pro-wordpress-plugin/21772657\">WPGYM</a> to accept payments via PayPal.</li>\n</ul><h3>12. <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/wp-membership/10066554\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">WP Membership</a>\n</h3><figure class=\"post_image\"><a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/wp-membership/10066554\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"WP Best Membership Plugin WordPress\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/2660/posts/35597/image/wp-membership-10066554.jpg\"></a></figure><p>As far as setting up your WordPress membership site is concerned, it doesn't get easier than with WP Membership. Just install this member list plugin for WordPress, and you're done. No extra configuration, no extended setup. It just works.</p><p>On top of the responsive design of this WordPress membership management plugin, WP offers features like:</p><ul>\n<li>Mailchimp integration for new members</li>\n<li>PayPal, Stripe, and WooCommerce payment gateway</li>\n<li>seven pricing tables</li>\n<li>coupon functionality</li>\n<li>11 language files included</li>\n</ul><h2>10 Free WordPress Membership Plugins for Download in 2020</h2><p>Maybe you only require access to a limited feature set, or you simply want to experiment with membership functionality before deciding whether this is the right direction for your website. </p><p>If you’re not ready to invest in a premium WordPress membership plugin just yet, then there are plenty of free plugins available. </p><p>Here, we’ve collected ten of our favourite free plugins from across the web: </p><h3>1. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/user-registration/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">User Registration</a>\n</h3><p>This lightweight plugin lets you quickly and easily build registration and login forms using drag and drop. </p><p>If you need additional functionality, then there are several premium addons available, including add-ons that enable social login, so users can register and log in to your website with their existing Facebook, Twitter, or LinkedIn credentials. You can also purchase add-ons that integrate the User Registration plugin with popular services such as WooCommerce, PayPal, and Mailchimp.</p><h3>2. <a href=\"https://en-gb.wordpress.org/plugins/ultimate-member/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Ultimate Member</a>\n</h3><p>The Ultimate Member plugin lets you create online communities and WordPress membership sites in <em>seconds</em> by automatically generating all the pages required to offer complete membership functionality, including user profiles, login, registration, and a password reset page. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"free WordPress Membership plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/ultimate-member-free-wordpress-plugin.jpg\"><figcaption>You can quickly and easily generate all the pages required to deliver a complete membership website, using the free Ultimate Member plugin.</figcaption></figure><p>If you need to modify any of Ultimate Member’s forms, then it also includes a drag-and-drop form editor that supports custom fields. By experimenting with custom fields, you can create an endless variety of unique login, registration, and profile editing forms. </p><h3>3. <a href=\"https://en-gb.wordpress.org/plugins/paid-memberships-pro/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Paid Memberships Pro</a>\n</h3><p>You can use this plugin to restrict access to your website’s content based on various membership levels, including one-time payments, recurring subscriptions, and custom trial periods. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"Paid Memberships Pro Free WordPress Membership Site Plugin\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/free-wordpress-plugins-Paid-Memberships-Pro.jpg\"><figcaption>The Paid Membership Pro plugin can add an additional revenue source to your website.</figcaption></figure><p>Whenever an unregistered user attempts to access restricted content, they’ll be automatically re-routed into a funnel that’s designed to drive them towards making a purchase. </p><p>Paid Memberships Pro supports PayPal, Stripe, Authorize.net, Braintree, and 2Checkout, and is used by over 80,000 organizations worldwide. </p><h3>4. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/simple-membership/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Simple Membership</a>\n</h3><p>This free WordPress plugin lets you protect your posts and pages so only registered members can view your content. Simple Membership also gives you the option to add Google reCAPTCHA to your registration, login, and password reset forms, and to require new users to accept your terms and conditions before they can register with your website. </p><p>If you need to customize the Simple Membership plugin, then it also provides lots of action and filter hooks, plus an API that you can use to query, create, and update member accounts. </p><h3>5. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/wp-members/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">WP-Members Membership Plugin</a>\n</h3><p>When you restrict content using the WP-Members Membership plugin, it automatically generates post excerpt teaser content, to encourage non-registered users to purchase a subscription. </p><p>If you’re a developer, then the free WP-Members Membership is also highly customizable. It provides over 120 action and filter hooks, plus a library of API functions. </p><h3>6. <a href=\"https://membership.supsystic.com/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Supsystic</a>\n</h3><p>This free WordPress plugin has everything you need to create a revenue-generating membership website. </p><p>The Supsystic plugin has a strong focus on social content, allowing members to connect via <strong>Follow</strong> and <strong>Add as friend</strong> features. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"Visitors can connect with other users join groups and perform other social media-inspired features \" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/best-free-membership-plugin-Supsystic.jpg\"><figcaption>Visitors can connect with other users, join groups, and perform other social media-inspired tasks. </figcaption></figure><p>You can also use the Supsystic plugin to create groups where like-minded members can engage with one another.</p><h3>7. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/users-ultra/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">Users Ultra Membership</a>\n</h3><p>This free WordPress membership management plugin can generate all the forms users need to register, log in, and manage their membership. If the default forms don’t fulfill all of your requirements, then you can add your own fields using the plugin’s Fields Customizer tool. </p><p>Users Ultra Membership offers lots of functionality that free plugins often reserve for their premium add-ons. Out of the box, Users Ultra Membership supports social login, integrates with Mailchimp, PayPal, and bbPress, and auto-syncs with WooCommerce.</p><h3>8. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/profilegrid-user-profiles-groups-and-communities/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">ProfileGrid</a>\n</h3><p>While ProfileGrid is fundamentally a membership plugin and profile builder, it also supports groups, so the people who visit your website can connect with other people who share their interests. These groups can be free or paid, public or private, and you can display different content depending on whether a user is signed up to a particular group. </p><p>ProfileGrid is also notable in that it offers a built-in private messaging system that works in real time, and it allows users to submit and share blogs via your membership website. </p><figure class=\"post_image\"><img alt=\"ProfileGrid supports user-submitted content including guest blogs\" src=\"https://cms-assets.tutsplus.com/uploads/users/369/posts/34344/image/ProfileGrid-guest-bloggers-wordpress.jpg\"><figcaption>ProfileGrid supports user-submitted content, including guest blogs.</figcaption></figure><h3>9. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/wp-user-frontend/\" target=\"_self\">WP User Frontend</a>\n</h3><p>This plugin features an easy-to-use drag-and-drop form builder. Once you’ve created your perfect form, you can place it anywhere on your website using the shortcode that WP User Frontend generates automatically. </p><p>WP User Frontend also allows registered users to post content on your website, so you could potentially create your own network of guest bloggers. Once a guest writer’s content is live on your site, they can go back and edit their work, and reply to comments, making this a great plugin for sparking discussion.</p><h3>10. <a href=\"https://wordpress.org/plugins/wp-user-manager/\" rel=\"external\" target=\"_blank\">WP User Manager</a>\n</h3><p>WP User Manager has everything you need to create a membership website, but some standout features include:</p><ul>\n<li>A handy shortcode editor that’s embedded within the WordPress page editor, so you no longer have to try to remember complicated shortcodes.</li>\n<li>A templating system that lets you customize all of this plugin’s templates.</li>\n<li>The ability to ban visitors from using certain usernames—perfect for ensuring no-one ever registers with your website using an offensive username!</li>\n</ul><p>WP User Manager also offers a free extension where users can request an export and erasure of their personal data, which can help ensure your website remains GDPR compliant. </p><h2>Create a Complete Membership Website in 2 Easy Steps</h2><p>If you want to create an online community, then WordPress plugins are the easiest way to add membership functionality to your website. Don’t believe me? In this section, I’ll show you how to generate all the pages required to create and support a flourishing online community on your WordPress membership site, in just two steps. </p><p>For this quick and easy tutorial, I’ll be demonstrating the <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/userpro-user-profiles-with-social-login/5958681?_ga=2.209361236.71199192.1575379090-1280144257.1574810364\">UserPro plugin</a>—if you opt for a different membership plugin then the steps may differ, but as long as you choose a <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/category/wordpress/membership\">professional, high-quality membership plugin</a>, the process should be just as easy.</p><h3>1. Install the UserPro Plugin</h3><p>Assuming you’ve already purchased and downloaded <a href=\"https://codecanyon.net/item/userpro-user-profiles-with-social-login/5958681?_ga=2.209361236.71199192.1575379090-1280144257.1574810364\">UserPro</a>, the first step is to upload this plugin to your WordPress website: </p><ul>\n<li>In WordPress’s left-hand menu, select <strong>Plugins &gt; Add New &gt; Upload plugin</strong>. </li>\n<li>Scroll to the <strong>If you have a plugin in a .zip format</strong> section and select <strong>Choose file</strong>. </li>\n<li>Select the <strong>userpro.zip</strong> file you downloaded as part of the purchasing process, and then click <strong>Install now</strong>.</li>\n<li>When prompted, select <strong>Activate plugin</strong>.</li>\n</ul><h3>2. Enter Your Purchase Code</h3><p>When you purchased UserPro, you should have received an email with the subject line <strong>[CodeCanyon] Purchase Confirmation</strong>. This email contains a purchase code that you’ll need to share with WordPress: </p><ul>\n<li>Open your purchase confirmation email and copy your unique <strong>Purchase code</strong>. </li>\n<li>In WordPress’s left-hand menu, select <strong>UserPro</strong>.</li>\n<li>Select the <strong>Licensing</strong> tab.</li>\n<li>Scroll to the <strong>Activate UserPro</strong> section and paste your purchase code into the accompanying text field. </li>\n<li>Select <strong>Save Changes</strong>. </li>\n</ul><h3>Your Community Website Is Ready to Go!</h3><p>And that’s it! UserPro has automatically created all the pages your new community website needs, including registration and login pages, fully editable user profiles, and a searchable directory of everyone who’s registered with your website. To check out this content for yourself, select <strong>Pages &gt; All Pages</strong> and spend some time exploring all the membership pages that UserPro has kindly created for you.</p><p>After just a few minutes’ work, you have everything you need to start your own online community, but UserPro has much more to offer, including giving visitors the option to register using their existing Twitter or Facebook credentials, and displaying the user's location via Google Maps integration. </p><p>If you want to learn more about this plugin, then check out our two-part UserPro series where I show you how to implement social login, add Google Maps integration, and much more.</p><ul class=\"roundup-block__contents posts--half-width roundup-block--card\">\n<li class=\"roundup-block__content
